<div id="product-commentary-overview-2" data-bn-widget-name="product-commentary-overview" class="box with-bullets product-commentary product-commentary-overview"><aside><div id="product-commentary-advertisement-1" data-bn-widget-name="product-commentary-advertisement" class="advertisement-layout product-commentary-advertisement"><div class="headerText">Advertising</div>
    <div class="ad"><div id="adsense-medium-rectangle-1" data-bn-widget-name="adsense-medium-rectangle" class="wide-300 tall-250  adsense adsense-medium-rectangle"><!-- this is where the ad goes --></div></div></div></aside>
        <section>
            

<div class="fade-to-more">
    <div class="content box">
        
                <h3>Overview</h3>
                <div class="simple-html">
                    <p>In ancient times the Rings of Power were crafted by the Elven-smiths, and Sauron, the Dark Lord, forged the One Ring, filling it with his own power so that he could rule all others. But the One Ring was taken from him, and though he sought it throughout Middle-earth, it remained lost to him. After many ages, it fell by chance into the hands of the hobbit Bilbo Baggins.</p>
<p>From his fastness in the Dark Tower of Mordor, Sauron's power spread far and wide. He gathered all the Great Rings to him, but always he searched for the One Ring that would complete his dominion.<br>
On Bilbo's eleventy-first birthday, he disappeared, bequeathing to his young cousin, Frodo, the Ruling Ring and a perilous quest: to journey across Middle-earth, deep into the shadow of the Dark Lord, and destroy the Ring by casting it into the Cracks of Doom.</p>
<p>The Lord of the Rings tells of the great quest undertaken by Frodo and the Fellowship of the Ring: Gandalf the Wizard; the hobbits Merry, Pippin, and Sam; Gimli the Dwarf; Legolas the Elf; Boromir of Gondor; and a tall, mysterious stranger called Strider.</p>


                        <p>Saga of a group heroes who set forth to save their world from evil. A brotherhood of 'hobbits,' elves, dwarves, and people is formed to combat evil forces. Tolkien drew on his extensive knowledge of folklore and the classics to create the worlds and creatures described in the trilogy, which includes <i>The Fellowship of the Ring</i>, <i>The Two Towers</i>, and <i>The Return of the King</i>.
</p>
                    
                </div>

            <aside><h5>More by this Author</h5>
                <ul>
                
                        <li><a href="http://www.barnesandnoble.com/w/the-hobbit-and-the-lord-of-the-rings-jrr-tolkien/1119268896?ean=9780544445789" >The Hobbit and The Lord of the Rings: Deluxe Pocket Boxed Set</a></li>

                        <li><a href="http://www.barnesandnoble.com/w/unfinished-tales-of-numenor-and-middle-earth-j-r-r-tolkien/1102154100?ean=9780544337992" >Unfinished Tales of Numenor and Middle-earth</a></li>

            <section>
                <div class="basic-info">
                <div class="contributor-bio has-image box">
                    
                        <div class="image"><img src="http://images.barnesandnoble.com/images/35700000/35705246.JPG" alt="J.R.R. Tolkien" /></div>
                    
                    <div class="bio">
                        

<div class="fade-to-more">
    <div class="content box">
        <p>J.R.R. TOLKIEN (1892â1973) is the creator of Middle-earth and author of such classic and extraordinary works of fiction as <b>The Hobbit, The Lord of the Rings,</b> and <b>The Silmarillion</b> . His books have been translated into more than fifty languages and have sold many millions of copies worldwide.</p>


                                <h3>Biography</h3>
                                John Ronald Reuel Tolkien was born on the 3rd January, 1892 at Bloemfontein in the Orange Free State, but at the age of four he and his brother were taken back to England by their mother. After his father's death the family moved to Sarehole, on the south-eastern edge of Birmingham. Tolkien spent a happy childhood in the countryside and his sensibility to the rural landscape can clearly be seen in his writing and his pictures. <p>    His mother died when he was only twelve and both he and his brother were made wards of the local priest and sent to King Edward's School, Birmingham, where Tolkien shine in his classical work. After completing a First in English Language and Literature at Oxford, Tolkien married Edith Bratt. He was also commissioned in the Lancashire Fusiliers and fought in the battle of the Somme. After the war, he obtained a post on the New English Dictionary and began to write the mythological and legendary cycle which he originally called "The Book of Lost Tales" but which eventually became known as <I>The Silmarillion</I>. <p>    In 1920 Tolkien was appointed Reader in English Language at the University of Leeds which was the beginning of a distinguished academic career culminating with his election as Rawlinson and Bosworth Professor of Anglo-Saxon at Oxford. Meanwhile Tolkien wrote for his children and told them the story of <I>The Hobbit</I>. It was his publisher, Stanley Unwin, who asked for a sequel to <I>The Hobbit</I> and gradually Tolkien wrote <I>The Lord of the Rings</I>, a huge story that took twelve years to complete and which was not published until Tolkien was approaching retirement. After retirement Tolkien and his wife lived near Oxford, but then moved to Bournemouth. Tolkien returned to Oxford after his wife's death in 1971. He died on 2 September 1973 leaving <I>The Silmarillion</I> to be edited for publication by his son, Christopher. <p>    <I>Author biography courtesy of HarperCollins (UK)</I>.<p>

                <h3>Read an Excerpt</h3>
                <div class="simple-html">THE LORD OF THE RINGS<br>
THE FELLOWSHIP OF THE RING<br>
BOOK ONE<br>
Chapter 1<br>
A Long-Expected Party<br>
When Mr. Bilbo Baggins of Bag End announced that he would shortly be <br>
celebrating his eleventy-first birthday with a party of special <br>
magnificence, there was much talk and excitement in Hobbiton.<br>
 Bilbo was very rich and very peculiar, and had been the <br>
wonder of the Shire for sixty years, ever since his remarkable <br>
disappearance and unexpected return. The riches he had brought back <br>
from his travels had now become a local legend, and it was popularly <br>
believed, whatever the old folk might say, that the Hill at Bag End <br>
was full of tunnels stuffed with treasure. And if that was not enough <br>
for fame, there was also his prolonged vigour to marvel at. Time wore <br>
on, but it seemed to have little effect on Mr. Baggins. At ninety he <br>
was much the same as at fifty. At ninety-nine they began to call him <br>
well-preserved; but unchanged would have been nearer the mark. There <br>
were some that shook their heads and thought this was too much of a <br>
good thing; it seemed unfair that anyone should possess (apparently) <br>
perpetual youth as well as (reputedly) inexhaustible wealth.<br>
 'It will have to be paid for,' they said. 'It isn't natural, <br>
and trouble will come of it!'<br>
<br>
 But so far trouble had not come; and as Mr. Baggins was <br>
generous with his money, most people were willing to forgive him his <br>
oddities and his good fortune. He remained on visiting terms with his <br>
relatives (except, of course, the Sackville-Bagginses), and he had <br>
many devoted admirers among the hobbits of poor and unimportant <br>
families. But he had no close friends, until some of his younger <br>
cousins began to grow up.<br>
 The eldest of these, and Bilbo's favourite, was young Frodo <br>
Baggins. When Bilbo was ninety-nine he adopted Frodo as his heir, and <br>
brought him to live at Bag End; and the hopes of the Sackville-<br>
Bagginses were finally dashed. Bilbo and Frodo happened to have the <br>
same birthday, September 22nd. 'You had better come and live here, <br>
Frodo my lad,' said Bilbo one day; 'and then we can celebrate our <br>
birthday-parties comfortably together.' At that time Frodo was still <br>
in his tweens, as the hobbits called the irresponsible twenties <br>
between childhood and coming of age at thirty-three.<br>
 Twelve more years passed. Each year the Bagginses had given <br>
very lively combined birthday-parties at Bag End; but now it was <br>
understood that something quite exceptional was being planned for <br>
that autumn. Bilbo was going to be eleventy-one, 111, a rather <br>
curious number, and a very respectable age for a hobbit (the Old Took <br>
himself had only reached 130); and Frodo was going to be thirty-<br>
three, 33, an important number: the date of his 'coming of age'.<br>
 Tongues began to wag in Hobbiton and Bywater; and rumour of <br>
the coming event travelled all over the Shire. The history and <br>
character of Mr. Bilbo Baggins became once again the chief topic of <br>
conversation; and the older folk suddenly found their reminiscences <br>
in welcome demand.<br>
 No one had a more attentive audience than old Ham Gamgee, <br>
commonly known as the Gaffer. He held forth at The Ivy Bush, a small <br>
inn on the Bywater road; and he spoke with some authority, for he had <br>
tended the garden at Bag End for forty years, and had helped old <br>
Holman in the same job before that. Now that he was himself growing <br>
old and stiff in the joints, the job was mainly carried on by his <br>
youngest son, Sam Gamgee. Both father and son were on very friendly <br>
terms with Bilbo and Frodo. They lived on the Hill itself, in Number <br>
3 Bagshot Row just below Bag End.<br>
 'A very nice well-spoken gentlehobbit is Mr. Bilbo, as I've <br>
always said,' the Gaffer declared. With perfect truth: for Bilbo was <br>
very polite to him, calling him 'Master Hamfast', and consulting him <br>
constantly upon the growing of vegetables â in the matter of 'roots', <br>
especially potatoes, the Gaffer was recognized as the leading <br>
authority by all in the neighbourhood (including himself).<br>
 'But what about this Frodo that lives with him?' asked Old <br>
Noakes of Bywater. 'Baggins is his name, but he's more than half a <br>
Brandybuck, they say. It beats me why any Baggins of Hobbiton should <br>
go looking for a wife away there in Buckland, where folks are so <br>
queer.'<br>
 'And no wonder they're queer,' put in Daddy Twofoot (the <br>
Gaffer's next-door neighbour), 'if they live on the wrong side of the <br>
Brandywine River, and right agin the Old Forest. That's a dark bad <br>
place, if half the tales be true.'<br>
 'You're right, Dad!' said the Gaffer. 'Not that the <br>
Brandybucks of Buckland live in the Old Forest; but they're a queer <br>
breed, seemingly. They fool about with boats on that big river â and <br>
that isn't natural. Small wonder that trouble came of it, I say. But <br>
be that as it may, Mr. Frodo is as nice a young hobbit as you could <br>
wish to meet. Very much like Mr. Bilbo, and in more than looks. After <br>
all his father was a Baggins. A decent respectable hobbit was Mr. <br>
Drogo Baggins; there was never much to tell of him, till he was <br>
drownded.'<br>
 'Drownded?' said several voices. They had heard this and <br>
other darker rumours before, of course; but hobbits have a passion <br>
for family history, and they were ready to hear it again.<br>
 'Well, so they say,' said the Gaffer. 'You see: Mr. Drogo, he <br>
married poor Miss Primula Brandybuck. She was our Mr. Bilbo's first <br>
cousin on the mother's side (her mother being the youngest of the Old <br>
Took's daughters); and Mr. Drogo was his second cousin. So Mr. Frodo <br>
is his first and second cousin, once removed either way, as the <br>
saying is, if you follow me. And Mr. Drogo was staying at Brandy Hall <br>
with his father-in-law, old Master Gorbadoc, as he often did after <br>
his marriage (him being partial to his vittles, and old Gorbadoc <br>
keeping a mighty generous table); and he went out boating on the <br>
Brandywine River; and he and his wife were drownded, and poor Mr. <br>
Frodo only a child and all.'<br>
 'I've heard they went on the water after dinner in the <br>
moonlight,' said Old Noakes; 'and it was Drogo's weight as sunk the <br>
boat.'<br>
 'And I heard she pushed him in, and he pulled her in after <br>
him,' said Sandyman, the Hobbiton miller.<br>
 'You shouldn't listen to all you hear, Sandyman,' said the <br>
Gaffer, who did not much like the miller. 'There isn't no call to go <br>
talking of pushing and pulling. Boats are quite tricky enough for <br>
those that sit still without looking further for the cause of <br>
trouble. Anyway: there was this Mr. Frodo left an orphan and <br>
stranded, as you might say, among those queer Bucklanders, being <br>
brought up anyhow in Brandy Hall. A regular warren, by all accounts. <br>
Old Master Gorbadoc never had fewer than a couple of hundred <br>
relations in the place. Mr. Bilbo never did a kinder deed than when <br>
he brought the lad back to live among decent folk.<br>
 'But I reckon it was a nasty shock for those Sackville-<br>
Bagginses. They thought they were going to get Bag End, that time <br>
when he went off and was thought to be dead. And then he comes back <br>
and orders them off; and he goes on living and living, and never <br>
looking a day older, bless him! And suddenly he produces an heir, and <br>
has all the papers made out proper. The Sackville-Bagginses won't <br>
never see the inside of Bag End now, or it is to be hoped not.'<br>
 'There's a tidy bit of money tucked away up there, I hear <br>
tell,' said a stranger, a visitor on business from Michel Delving in <br>
the Westfarthing. 'All the top of your hill is full of tunnels packed <br>
with chests of gold and silver, and jools, by what I've heard.'<br>
 'Then you've heard more than I can speak to,' answered the <br>
Gaffer. I know nothing about jools. Mr. Bilbo is free with his money, <br>
and there seems no lack of it; but I know of no tunnel-making. I saw <br>
Mr. Bilbo when he came back, a matter of sixty years ago, when I was <br>
a lad. I'd not long come prentice to old Holman (him being my dad's <br>
cousin), but he had me up at Bag End helping him to keep folks from <br>
trampling and trapessing all over the garden while the sale was on. <br>
And in the middle of it all Mr. Bilbo comes up the Hill with a pony <br>
and some mighty big bags and a couple of chests. I don't doubt they <br>
were mostly full of treasure he had picked up in foreign parts, where <br>
there be mountains of gold, they say; but there wasn't enough to fill <br>
tunnels. But my lad Sam will know more about that. He's in and out of <br>
Bag End. Crazy about stories of the old days he is, and he listens to <br>
all Mr. Bilbo's tales. Mr. Bilbo has learned him his letters â <br>
meaning no harm, mark you, and I hope no harm will come of it.<br>
 'Elves and Dragons! I says to him. 'Cabbages and potatoes are <br>
better for me and you. Don't go getting mixed up in the business of <br>
your betters, or you'll land in trouble too big for you,' I says to <br>
him. And I might say it to others,' he added with a look at the <br>
stranger and the miller.<br>
 But the Gaffer did not convince his audience. The legend of <br>
Bilbo's wealth was now too firmly fixed in the minds of the younger <br>
generation of hobbits.<br>
 'Ah, but he has likely enough been adding to what he brought <br>
at first,' argued the miller, voicing common opinion. 'He's often <br>
away from home. And look at the outlandish folk that visit him: <br>
dwarves coming at night, and that old wandering conjuror, Gandalf, <br>
and all. You can say what you like, Gaffer, but Bag End's a queer <br>
place, and its folk are queerer.'<br>
 'And you can say what you like, about what you know no more <br>
of than you do of boating, Mr. Sandyman,' retorted the Gaffer, <br>
disliking the miller even more than usual. 'If that's being queer, <br>
then we could do with a bit more queerness in these parts. There's <br>
some not far away that wouldn't offer a pint of beer to a friend, if <br>
they lived in a hole with golden walls. But they do things proper at <br>
Bag End. Our Sam says that everyone's going to be invited to the <br>
party, and there's going to be presents, mark you, presents for all â <br>
this very month as is.'<br>
 That very month was September, and as fine as you could ask. <br>
A day or two later a rumour (probably started by the knowledgeable <br>
Sam) was spread about that there were going to be fireworks â <br>
fireworks, what is more, such as had not been seen in the Shire for <br>
nigh on a century, not indeed since the Old Took died.<br>
 Days passed and The Day drew nearer. An odd-looking waggon <br>
laden with odd-looking packages rolled into Hobbiton one evening and <br>
toiled up the Hill to Bag End. The startled hobbits peered out of <br>
lamplit doors to gape at it. It was driven by outlandish folk, <br>
singing strange songs: dwarves with long beards and deep hoods. A few <br>
of them remained at Bag End. At the end of the second week in <br>
September a cart came in through Bywater from the direction of the <br>
Brandywine Bridge in broad daylight. An old man was driving it all <br>
alone. He wore a tall pointed blue hat, a long grey cloak, and a <br>
silver scarf. He had a long white beard and bushy eyebrows that stuck <br>
out beyond the brim of his hat. Small hobbit-children ran after the <br>
cart all through Hobbiton and right up the hill. It had a cargo of <br>
fireworks, as they rightly guessed. At Bilbo's front door the old man <br>
began to unload: there were great bundles of fireworks of all sorts <br>
and shapes, each labelled with a large red G and the elf-rune, .<br>
 That was Gandalf's mark, of course, and the old man was <br>
Gandalf the Wizard, whose fame in the Shire was due mainly to his <br>
skill with fires, smokes, and lights. His real business was far more <br>
difficult and dangerous, but the Shire-folk knew nothing about it. To <br>
them he was just one of the 'attractions' at the Party. Hence the <br>
excitement of the hobbit-children. 'G for Grand!' they shouted, and <br>
the old man smiled. They knew him by sight, though he only appeared <br>
in Hobbiton occasionally and never stopped long; but neither they nor <br>
any but the oldest of their elders had seen one of his firework <br>
displays â they now belonged to the legendary past.<br>
 When the old man, helped by Bilbo and some dwarves, had <br>
finished unloading. Bilbo gave a few pennies away; but not a single <br>
squib or cracker was forthcoming, to the disappointment of the <br>
onlookers.<br>
 'Run away now!' said Gandalf. 'You will get plenty when the <br>
time comes.' Then he disappeared inside with Bilbo, and the door was <br>
shut. The young hobbits stared at the door in vain for a while, and <br>
then made off, feeling that the day of the party would never come.<br>
 Inside Bag End, Bilbo and Gandalf were sitting at the open <br>
window of a small room looking out west on to the garden. The late <br>
afternoon was bright and peaceful. The flowers glowed red and golden: <br>
snap-dragons and sun-flowers, and nasturtiums trailing all over the <br>
turf walls and peeping in at the round windows.<br>
 'How bright your garden looks!' said Gandalf.<br>
 'Yes,' said Bilbo. 'I am very fond indeed of it, and of all <br>
the dear old Shire; but I think I need a holiday.'<br>
 'You mean to go on with your plan then?'<br>
 'I do. I made up my mind months ago, and I haven't changed <br>
it.'<br>
 'Very well. It is no good saying any more. Stick to your <br>
plan â your whole plan, mind â and I hope it will turn out for the <br>
best, for you, and for all of us.'<br>
 'I hope so. Anyway I mean to enjoy myself on Thursday, and <br>
have my little joke.'<br>
 'Who will laugh, I wonder?' said Gandalf, shaking his head.<br>
 'We shall see,' said Bilbo.<br>
 The next day more carts rolled up the Hill, and still more <br>
carts. There might have been some grumbling about 'dealing locally', <br>
but that very week orders began to pour out of Bag End for every kind <br>
of provision, commodity, or luxury that could be obtained in Hobbiton <br>
or Bywater or anywhere in the neighbourhood. People became <br>
enthusiastic; and they began to tick off the days on the calendar; <br>
and they watched eagerly for the postman, hoping for invitations.<br>
 Before long the invitations began pouring out, and the <br>
Hobbiton post-office was blocked, and the Bywater post-office was <br>
snowed under, and voluntary assistant postmen were called for. There <br>
was a constant stream of them going up the Hill, carrying hundreds of <br>
polite variations on Thank you, I shall certainly come.<br>
 A notice appeared on the gate at Bag End: NO ADMITTANCE <br>
EXCEPT ON PARTY BUSINESS. Even those who had, or pretended to have <br>
Party Business were seldom allowed inside. Bilbo was busy: writing <br>
invitations, ticking off answers, packing up presents, and making <br>
some private preparations of his own. From the time of Gandalf's <br>
arrival he remained hidden from view.<br>
 One morning the hobbits woke to find the large field, south <br>
of Bilbo's front door, covered with ropes and poles for tents and <br>
pavilions. A special entrance was cut into the bank leading to the <br>
road, and wide steps and a large white gate were built there. The <br>
three hobbit-families of Bagshot Row, adjoining the field, were <br>
intensely interested and generally envied. Old Gaffer Gamgee stopped <br>
even pretending to work in his garden.<br>
 The tents began to go up. There was a specially large <br>
pavilion, so big that the tree that grew in the field was right <br>
inside it, and stood proudly near one end, at the head of the chief <br>
table. Lanterns were hung on all its branches. More promising still <br>
(to the hobbits' mind): an enormous open-air kitchen was erected in <br>
the north corner of the field. A draught of cooks, from every inn and <br>
eating-house for miles around, arrived to supplement the dwarves and <br>
other odd folk that were quartered at Bag End. Excitement rose to its <br>
height.<br>
 Then the weather clouded over. That was on Wednesday the eve <br>
of the Party. Anxiety was intense. Then Thursday, September the 22nd, <br>
actually dawned. The sun got up, the clouds vanished, flags were <br>
unfurled and the fun began.<br>
 Bilbo Baggins called it a party, but it was really a variety <br>
of entertainments rolled into one. Practically everybody living near <br>
was invited. A very few were overlooked by accident, but as they <br>
turned up all the same, that did not matter. Many people from other <br>
parts of the Shire were also asked; and there were even a few from <br>
outside the borders. Bilbo met the guests (and additions) at the new <br>
white gate in person. He gave away presents to all and sundry â the <br>
latter were those who went out again by a back way and came in again <br>
by the gate. Hobbits give presents to other people on their own <br>
birthdays. Not very expensive ones, as a rule, and not so lavishly as <br>
on this occasion; but it was not a bad system. Actually in Hobbiton <br>
and Bywater every day in the year it was somebody's birthday, so that <br>
every hobbit in those parts had a fair chance of at least one present <br>
at least once a week. But they never got tired of them.<br>
 On this occasion the presents were unusually good. The hobbit-<br>
children were so excited that for a while they almost forgot about <br>
eating. There were toys the like of which they had never seen before, <br>
all beautiful and some obviously magical. Many of them had indeed <br>
been ordered a year before, and had come all the way from the <br>
Mountain and from Dale, and were of real dwarf-make.<br>
 When every guest had been welcomed and was finally inside the <br>
gate, there were songs, dances, music, games, and, of course, food <br>
and drink. There were three official meals: lunch, tea, and dinner <br>
(or supper). But lunch and tea were marked chiefly by the fact that <br>
at those times all the guests were sitting down and eating together. <br>
At other times there were merely lots of people eating and drinking â<br>
 continuously from elevenses until six-thirty, when the fireworks <br>
started.<br>
 The fireworks were by Gandalf: they were not only brought by <br>
him, but designed and made by him; and the special effects, set <br>
pieces, and flights of rockets were let off by him. But there was <br>
also a generous distribution of squibs, crackers, backarappers, <br>
sparklers, torches, dwarf-candles, elf-fountains, goblin-barkers and <br>
thunder-claps. They were all superb. The art of Gandalf improved with <br>
age.<br>
 There were rockets like a flight of scintillating birds <br>
singing with sweet voices. There were green trees with trunks of dark <br>
smoke: their leaves opened like a whole spring unfolding in a moment, <br>
and their shining branches dropped glowing flowers down upon the <br>
astonished hobbits, disappearing with a sweet scent just before they <br>
touched their upturned faces. There were fountains of butterflies <br>
that flew glittering into the trees; there were pillars of coloured <br>
fires that rose and turned into eagles, or sailing ships, or a <br>
phalanx of flying swans; there was a red thunderstorm and a shower of <br>
yellow rain; there was a forest of silver spears that sprang suddenly <br>
into the air with a yell like an embattled army, and came down again <br>
into the Water with a hiss like a hundred hot snakes. And there was <br>
also one last surprise, in honour of Bilbo, and it startled the <br>
hobbits exceedingly, as Gandalf intended. The lights went out. A <br>
great smoke went up. It shaped itself like a mountain seen in the <br>
distance, and began to glow at the summit. It spouted green and <br>
scarlet flames. Out flew a red-golden dragon â not life-size, but <br>
terribly life-like: fire came from his jaws, his eyes glared down; <br>
there was a roar, and he whizzed three times over the heads of the <br>
crowd. They all ducked, and many fell flat on their faces. The dragon <br>
passed like an express train, turned a somersault, and burst over <br>
Bywater with a deafening explosion.<br>
 'That is the signal for supper!' said Bilbo. The pain and <br>
alarm vanished at once, and the prostrate hobbits leaped to their <br>
feet. There was a splendid supper for everyone; for everyone, that <br>
is, except those invited to the special family dinner-party. This was <br>
held in the great pavilion with the tree. The invitations were <br>
limited to twelve dozen (a number also called by the hobbits one <br>
Gross, though the word was not considered proper to use of people); <br>
and the guests were selected from all the families to which Bilbo and <br>
Frodo were related, with the addition of a few special unrelated <br>
friends (such as Gandalf). Many young hobbits were included, and <br>
present by parental permission; for hobbits were easy-going with <br>
their children in the matter of sitting up late, especially when <br>
there was a chance of getting them a free meal. Bringing up young <br>
hobbits took a lot of provender.<br>
 There were many Bagginses and Boffins, and also many Tooks <br>
and Brandybucks; there were various Grubbs (relations of Bilbo <br>
Baggins' grandmother), and various Chubbs (connexions of his Took <br>
grandfather); and a selection of Burrowses, Bolgers, Bracegirdles, <br>
Brockhouses, Goodbodies, Hornblowers and Proudfoots. Some of these <br>
were only very distantly connected with Bilbo, and some of them had <br>
hardly ever been in Hobbiton before, as they lived in remote corners <br>
of the Shire. The Sackville-Bagginses were not forgotten. Otho and <br>
his wife Lobelia were present. They disliked Bilbo and detested <br>
Frodo, but so magnificent was the invitation card, written in golden <br>
ink, that they had felt it was impossible to refuse. Besides, their <br>
cousin, Bilbo, had been specializing in food for many years and his <br>
table had a high reputation.<br>
 All the one hundred and forty-four guests expected a pleasant <br>
feast; though they rather dreaded the after-dinner speech of their <br>
host (an inevitable item). He was liable to drag in bits of what he <br>
called poetry; and sometimes, after a glass or two, would allude to <br>
the absurd adventures of his mysterious journey. The guests were not <br>
disappointed: they had a very pleasant feast, in fact an engrossing <br>
entertainment: rich, abundant, varied, and prolonged. The purchase of <br>
provisions fell almost to nothing throughout the district in the <br>
ensuing weeks; but as Bilbo's catering had depleted the stocks of <br>
most stores, cellars and warehouses for miles around, that did not <br>
matter much.<br>
 After the feast (more or less) came the Speech. Most of the <br>
company were, however, now in a tolerant mood, at that delightful <br>
stage which they called 'filling up the corners'. They were sipping <br>
their favourite drinks, and nibbling at their favourite dainties, and <br>
their fears were forgotten. They were prepared to listen to anything, <br>
and to cheer at every full stop.<br>
 My dear People, began Bilbo, rising in his place. 'Hear! <br>
Hear! Hear!' they shouted, and kept on repeating it in chorus, <br>
seeming reluctant to follow their own advice. Bilbo left his place <br>
and went and stood on a chair under the illuminated tree. The light <br>
of the lanterns fell on his beaming face; the golden buttons shone on <br>
his embroidered silk waistcoat. They could all see him standing, <br>
waving one hand in the air, the other was in his trouser-pocket.<br>
 My dear Bagginses and Boffins, he began again; and my dear <br>
Tooks and Brandybucks, and Grubbs, and Chubbs, and Burrowses, and <br>
Hornblowers, and Bolgers, Bracegirdles, Goodbodies, Brockhouses and <br>
Proudfoots. 'ProudFEET!' shouted an elderly hobbit from the back of <br>
the pavilion. His name, of course, was Proudfoot, and well merited; <br>
his feet were large, exceptionally furry, and both were on the table.<br>
 Proudfoots, repeated Bilbo. Also my good Sackville-Bagginses <br>
that I welcome back at last to Bag End. Today is my one hundred and <br>
eleventh birthday: I am eleventy-one today! 'Hurray! Hurray! Many <br>
Happy Returns!' they shouted, and they hammered joyously on the <br>
tables. Bilbo was doing splendidly. This was the sort of stuff they <br>
liked: short and obvious.<br>
 I hope you are all enjoying yourselves as much as I am. <br>
Deafening cheers. Cries of Yes (and No). Noises of trumpets and <br>
horns, pipes and flutes, and other musical instruments. There were, <br>
as has been said, many young hobbits present. Hundreds of musical <br>
crackers had been pulled. Most of them bore the mark DALE on them; <br>
which did not convey much to most of the hobbits, but they all agreed <br>
they were marvellous crackers. They contained instruments, small, but <br>
of perfect make and enchanting tones. Indeed, in one corner some of <br>
the young Tooks and Brandybucks, supposing Uncle Bilbo to have <br>
finished (since he had plainly said all that was necessary), now got <br>
up an impromptu orchestra, and began a merry dance-tune. Master <br>
Everard Took and Miss Melilot Brandybuck got on a table and with <br>
bells in their hands began to dance the Springle-ring: a pretty <br>
dance, but rather vigorous.<br>
 But Bilbo had not finished. Seizing a horn from a youngster <br>
near by, he blew three loud hoots. The noise subsided. I shall not <br>
keep you long, he cried. Cheers from all the assembly. I have called <br>
you all together for a Purpose. Something in the way that he said <br>
this made an impression. There was almost silence, and one or two of <br>
the Tooks pricked up their ears.<br>
 Indeed, for Three Purposes! First of all, to tell you that I <br>
am immensely fond of you all, and that eleventy-one years is too <br>
short a time to live among such excellent and admirable hobbits. <br>
Tremendous outburst of approval.<br>
 I don't know half of you half as well as I should like; and I <br>
like less than half of you half as well as you deserve. This was <br>
unexpected and rather difficult. There was some scattered clapping, <br>
but most of them were trying to work it out and see if it came to a <br>
compliment.<br>
 Secondly, to celebrate my birthday. Cheers again. I should <br>
say: OUR birthday. For it is, of course, also the birthday of my heir <br>
and nephew, Frodo. He comes of age and into his inheritance today. <br>
Some perfunctory clapping by the elders; and some loud shouts <br>
of 'Frodo! Frodo! Jolly old Frodo,' from the juniors. The Sackville-<br>
Bagginses scowled, and wondered what was meant by 'coming into his <br>
inheritance'. <br>
 Together we score one hundred and forty-four. Your numbers <br>
were chosen to fit this remarkable total: One Gross, if I may use the <br>
expression. No cheers. This was ridiculous. Many of his guests, and <br>
especially the Sackville-Bagginses, were insulted, feeling sure they <br>
had only been asked to fill up the required number, like goods in a <br>
package. 'One Gross, indeed! Vulgar expression.'<br>
 It is also, if I may be allowed to refer to ancient history, <br>
the anniversary of my arrival by barrel at Esgaroth on the Long Lake; <br>
though the fact that it was my birthday slipped my memory on that <br>
occasion. I was only fifty-one then, and birthdays did not seem so <br>
important. The banquet was very splendid, however, though I had a bad <br>
cold at the time, I remember, and could only say 'thag you very <br>
buch'. I now repeat it more correctly: Thank you very much for coming <br>
to my little party. Obstinate silence. They all feared that a song or <br>
some poetry was now imminent; and they were getting bored. Why <br>
couldn't he stop talking and let them drink his health? But Bilbo did <br>
not sing or recite. He paused for a moment.<br>
 Thirdly and finally, he said, I wish to make an ANNOUNCEMENT. <br>
He spoke this last word so loudly and suddenly that everyone sat up <br>
who still could. I regret to announce that â though, as I said, <br>
eleventy-one years is far too short a time to spend among you â this <br>
is the END. I am going. I am leaving NOW. GOOD-BYE!<br>
<br>
 He stepped down and vanished. There was a blinding flash of <br>
light, and the guests all blinked. When they opened their eyes Bilbo <br>
was nowhere to be seen. One hundred and forty-four flabbergasted <br>
hobbits sat back speechless. Old Odo Proudfoot removed his feet from <br>
the table and stamped. Then there was a dead silence, until suddenly, <br>
after several deep breaths, every Baggins, Boffin, Took, Brandybuck, <br>
Grubb, Chubb, Burrows, Bolger, Bracegirdle, Brockhouse, Goodbody, <br>
Hornblower, and Proudfoot began to talk at once.<br>
 It was generally agreed that the joke was in very bad taste, <br>
and more food and drink were needed to cure the guests of shock and <br>
annoyance. 'He's mad. I always said so,' was probably the most <br>
popular comment. Even the Tooks (with a few exceptions) thought <br>
Bilbo's behaviour was absurd. For the moment most of them took it for <br>
granted that his disappearance was nothing more than a ridiculous <br>
prank.<br>
 But old Rory Brandybuck was not so sure. Neither age nor an <br>
enormous dinner had clouded his wits, and he said to his daughter-in-<br>
law, Esmeralda: 'There's something fishy in this, my dear! I believe <br>
that mad Baggins is off again. Silly old fool. But why worry? He <br>
hasn't taken the vittles with him.' He called loudly to Frodo to send <br>
the wine round again.<br>
 Frodo was the only one present who had said nothing. For some <br>
time he had sat silent beside Bilbo's empty chair, and ignored all <br>
remarks and questions. He had enjoyed the joke, of course, even <br>
though he had been in the know. He had difficulty in keeping from <br>
laughter at the indignant surprise of the guests. But at the same <br>
time he felt deeply troubled: he realized suddenly that he loved the <br>
old hobbit dearly. Most of the guests went on eating and drinking and <br>
discussing Bilbo Baggins' oddities, past and present; but the <br>
Sackville-Bagginses had already departed in wrath. Frodo did not want <br>
to have any more to do with the party. He gave orders for more wine <br>
to be served; then he got up and drained his own glass silently to <br>
the health of Bilbo, and slipped out of the pavilion.<br>
<br>
 As for Bilbo Baggins, even while he was making his speech, he <br>
had been fingering the golden ring in his pocket: his magic ring that <br>
he had kept secret for so many years. As he stepped down he slipped <br>
it on his finger, and he was never seen by any hobbit in Hobbiton <br>
again.<br>
 He walked briskly back to his hole, and stood for a moment <br>
listening with a smile to the din in the pavilion and to the sounds <br>
of merrymaking in other parts of the field. Then he went in. He took <br>
off his party clothes, folded up and wrapped in tissue-paper his <br>
embroidered silk waistcoat, and put it away. Then he put on quickly <br>
some old untidy garments, and fastened round his waist a worn leather <br>
belt. On it he hung a short sword in a battered black-leather <br>
scabbard. From a locked drawer, smelling of moth-balls, he took out <br>
an old cloak and hood. They had been locked up as if they were very <br>
precious, but they were so patched and weatherstained that their <br>
original colour could hardly be guessed: it might have been dark <br>
green. They were rather too large for him. He then went into his <br>
study, and from a large strong-box took out a bundle wrapped in old <br>
cloths, and a leather-bound manuscript; and also a large bulky <br>
envelope. The book and bundle he stuffed into the top of a heavy bag <br>
that was standing there, already nearly full. Into the envelope he <br>
slipped his golden ring, and its fine chain, and then sealed it, and <br>
addressed it to Frodo. At first he put it on the mantelpiece, but <br>
suddenly he removed it and stuck it in his pocket. At that moment the <br>
door opened and Gandalf came quickly in.<br>
 'Hullo!' said Bilbo. 'I wondered if you would turn up.'<br>
 'I am glad to find you visible,' replied the wizard, sitting <br>
down in a chair. 'I wanted to catch you and have a few final words. I <br>
suppose you feel that everything has gone off splendidly and <br>
according to plan?'<br>
 'Yes, I do,' said Bilbo. 'Though that flash was surprising: <br>
it quite startled me, let alone the others. A little addition of your <br>
own, I suppose?'<br>
 'It was. You have wisely kept that ring secret all these <br>
years, and it seemed to me necessary to give your guests something <br>
else that would seem to explain your sudden vanishment.'<br>
 'And would spoil my joke. You are an interfering old <br>
busybody,' laughed Bilbo, 'but I expect you know best, as usual.'<br>
 'I do â when I know anything. But I don't feel too sure about <br>
this whole affair. It has now come to the final point. You have had <br>
your joke, and alarmed or offended most of your relations, and given <br>
the whole Shire something to talk about for nine days, or ninety-nine <br>
more likely. Are you going any further?'<br>
 'Yes, I am. I feel I need a holiday, a very long holiday, as <br>
I have told you before. Probably a permanent holiday: I don't expect <br>
I shall return. In fact, I don't mean to, and I have made all <br>
arrangements.<br>
 'I am old, Gandalf. I don't look it, but I am beginning to <br>
feel it in my heart of hearts. Well-preserved indeed!' he <br>
snorted. 'Why, I feel all thin, sort of stretched, if you know what I <br>
mean: like butter that has been scraped over too much bread. That <br>
can't be right. I need a change, or something.'<br>
 Gandalf looked curiously and closely at him. 'No, it does not <br>
seem right,' he said thoughtfully. 'No, after all I believe your plan <br>
is probably the best.'<br>
 'Well, I've made up my mind, anyway. I want to see mountains <br>
again, Gandalf â mountains; and then find somewhere where I can rest. <br>
In peace and quiet, without a lot of relatives prying around, and a <br>
string of confounded visitors hanging on the bell. I might find <br>
somewhere where I can finish my book. I have thought of a nice ending <br>
for it: and he lived happily ever after to the end of his days.'<br>
 Gandalf laughed. 'I hope he will. But nobody will read the <br>
book, however it ends.'<br>
 'Oh, they may, in years to come. Frodo has read some already, <br>
as far as it has gone. You'll keep an eye on Frodo, won't you?'<br>
 'Yes, I will â two eyes, as often as I can spare them.'<br>
 'He would come with me, of course, if I asked him. In fact he <br>
offered to once, just before the party. But he does not really want <br>
to, yet. I want to see the wild country again before I die, and the <br>
Mountains; but he is still in love with the Shire, with woods and <br>
fields and little rivers. He ought to be comfortable here. I am <br>
leaving everything to him, of course, except a few oddments. I hope <br>
he will be happy, when he gets used to being on his own. It's time he <br>
was his own master now.'<br>
 'Everything?' said Gandalf. 'The ring as well? You agreed to <br>
that, you remember.'<br>
 'Well, er, yes, I suppose so,' stammered Bilbo.<br>
 'Where is it?'<br>
 'In an envelope, if you must know,' said Bilbo <br>
impatiently. 'There on the mantelpiece. Well, no! Here it is in my <br>
pocket!' He hesitated. 'Isn't that odd now?' he said softly to <br>
himself. 'Yet after all, why not? Why shouldn't it stay there?'<br>
 Gandalf looked again very hard at Bilbo, and there was a <br>
gleam in his eyes. 'I think, Bilbo,' he said quietly, 'I should leave <br>
it behind. Don't you want to?'<br>
 'Well yes â and no. Now it comes to it, I don't like parting <br>
with it at all, I may say. And I don't really see why I should. Why <br>
do you want me to?' he asked, and a curious change came over his <br>
voice. It was sharp with suspicion and annoyance. 'You are always <br>
badgering me about my ring; but you have never bothered me about the <br>
other things that I got on my journey.'<br>
 'No, but I had to badger you,' said Gandalf. 'I wanted the <br>
truth. It was important. Magic rings are â well, magical; and they <br>
are rare and curious. I was professionally interested in your ring, <br>
you may say; and I still am. I should like to know where it is, if <br>
you go wandering again. Also I think you have had it quite long <br>
enough. You won't need it any more, Bilbo, unless I am quite <br>
mistaken.'<br>
 Bilbo flushed, and there was an angry light in his eyes. His <br>
kindly face grew hard. 'Why not?' he cried. 'And what business is it <br>
of yours, anyway, to know what I do with my own things? It is my own. <br>
I found it. It came to me.'<br>
 'Yes, yes,' said Gandalf. 'But there is no need to get angry.'<br>
 'If I am it is your fault,' said Bilbo. 'It is mine, I tell <br>
you. My own. My precious. Yes, my precious.'<br>
 The wizard's face remained grave and attentive, and only a <br>
flicker in his deep eyes showed that he was startled and indeed <br>
alarmed. 'It has been called that before,' he said, 'but not by you.'<br>
 'But I say it now. And why not? Even if Gollum said the same <br>
once. It's not his now, but mine. And I shall keep it, I say.'<br>
 Gandalf stood up. He spoke sternly. 'You will be a fool if <br>
you do, Bilbo,' he said. 'You make that clearer with every word you <br>
say. It has got far too much hold on you. Let it go! And then you can <br>
go yourself, and be free.'<br>
 'I'll do as I choose and go as I please,' said Bilbo <br>
obstinately.<br>
 'Now, now, my dear hobbit!' said Gandalf. 'All your long life <br>
we have been friends, and you owe me something. Come! Do as you <br>
promised: give it up!'<br>
 'Well, if you want my ring yourself, say so!' cried <br>
Bilbo. 'But you won't get it. I won't give my precious away, I tell <br>
you.' His hand strayed to the hilt of his small sword.<br>
 Gandalf's eyes flashed. 'It will be my turn to get angry <br>
soon,' he said. 'If you say that again, I shall. Then you will see <br>
Gandalf the Grey uncloaked.' He took a step towards the hobbit, and <br>
he seemed to grow tall and menacing; his shadow filled the little <br>
room.<br>
 Bilbo backed away to the wall, breathing hard, his hand <br>
clutching at his pocket. They stood for a while facing one another, <br>
and the air of the room tingled. Gandalf's eyes remained bent on the <br>
hobbit. Slowly his hands relaxed, and he began to tremble.<br>
 'I don't know what has come over you, Gandalf,' he said. 'You <br>
have never been like this before. What is it all about? It is mine <br>
isn't it? I found it, and Gollum would have killed me, if I hadn't <br>
kept it. I'm not a thief, whatever he said.'<br>
 'I have never called you one,' Gandalf answered. 'And I am <br>
not one either. I am not trying to rob you, but to help you. I wish <br>
you would trust me, as you used.' He turned away, and the shadow <br>
passed. He seemed to dwindle again to an old grey man, bent and <br>
troubled.<br>
 Bilbo drew his hand over his eyes. 'I am sorry,' he <br>
said. 'But I felt so queer. And yet it would be a relief in a way not <br>
to be bothered with it any more. It has been so growing on my mind <br>
lately. Sometimes I have felt it was like an eye looking at me. And I <br>
am always wanting to put it on and disappear, don't you know; or <br>
wondering if it is safe, and pulling it out to make sure. I tried <br>
locking it up, but I found I couldn't rest without it in my pocket. I <br>
don't know why. And I don't seem able to make up my mind.'<br>
 'Then trust mine,' said Gandalf. 'It is quite made up. Go <br>
away and leave it behind. Stop possessing it. Give it to Frodo, and I <br>
will look after him.'<br>
 Bilbo stood for a moment tense and undecided. Presently he <br>
sighed. 'All right,' he said with an effort. 'I will.' Then he <br>
shrugged his shoulders, and smiled rather ruefully. 'After all that's <br>
what this party business was all about, really: to give away lots of <br>
birthday presents, and somehow make it easier to give it away at the <br>
same time. It hasn't made it any easier in the end, but it would be a <br>
pity to waste all my preparations. It would quite spoil the joke.'<br>
 'Indeed it would take away the only point I ever saw in the <br>
affair,' said Gandalf.<br>
 'Very well,' said Bilbo, 'it goes to Frodo with all the <br>
rest.' He drew a deep breath. 'And now I really must be starting, or <br>
somebody else will catch me. I have said good-bye, and I couldn't <br>
bear to do it all over again.' He picked up his bag and moved to the <br>
door.<br>
 'You have still got the ring in your pocket,' said the wizard.<br>
 'Well, so I have!' cried Bilbo. 'And my will and all the <br>
other documents too. You had better take it and deliver it for me. <br>
That will be safest.'<br>
 'No, don't give the ring to me,' said Gandalf. 'Put it on the <br>
mantelpiece. It will be safe enough there, till Frodo comes. I shall <br>
wait for him.'<br>
 Bilbo took out the envelope, but just as he was about to set <br>
it by the clock, his hand jerked back, and the packet fell on the <br>
floor. Before he could pick it up, the wizard stooped and seized it <br>
and set it in its place. A spasm of anger passed swiftly over the <br>
hobbit's face again. Suddenly it gave way to a look of relief and a <br>
laugh.<br>
 'Well, that's that,' he said. 'Now I'm off!'<br>
 They went out into the hall. Bilbo chose his favourite stick <br>
from the stand; then he whistled. Three dwarves came out of different <br>
rooms where they had been busy.<br>
 'Is everything ready?' asked Bilbo. 'Everything packed and <br>
labelled?'<br>
 'Everything,' they answered.<br>
 'Well, let's start then!' He stepped out of the front-door.<br>
 It was a fine night, and the black sky was dotted with stars. <br>
He looked up, sniffing the air. 'What fun! What fun to be off again, <br>
off on the Road with dwarves! This is what I have really been longing <br>
for, for years! Good-bye!' he said, looking at his old home and <br>
bowing to the door. 'Good-bye, Gandalf!'<br>
 'Good-bye, for the present, Bilbo. Take care of yourself! You <br>
are old enough, and perhaps wise enough.'<br>
 'Take care! I don't care. Don't you worry about me! I am as <br>
happy now as I have ever been, and that is saying a great deal. But <br>
the time has come. I am being swept off my feet at last,' he added, <br>
and then in a low voice, as if to himself, he sang softly in the dark:<br>
The Road goes ever on and on<br>
 Down from the door where it began.<br>
Now far ahead the Road has gone,<br>
 And I must follow, if I can,<br>
Pursuing it with eager feet,<br>
 Until it joins some larger way<br>
Where many paths and errands meet.<br>
 And whither then? I cannot say.<br>
 He paused, silent for a moment. Then without another word he <br>
turned away from the lights and voices in the fields and tents, and <br>
followed by his three companions went round into his garden, and <br>
trotted down the long sloping path. He jumped over a low place in the <br>
hedge at the bottom, and took to the meadows, passing into the night <br>
like a rustle of wind in the grass.<br>
 Gandalf remained for a while staring after him into the <br>
darkness. 'Goodbye, my dear Bilbo â until our next meeting!' he said <br>
softly and went back indoors.<br>
<br>
 Frodo came in soon afterwards, and found him sitting in the <br>
dark, deep in thought. 'Has he gone?' he asked.<br>
 'Yes,' answered Gandalf, 'he has gone at last.'<br>
 'I wish â I mean, I hoped until this evening that it was only <br>
a joke,' said Frodo. 'But I knew in my heart that he really meant to <br>
go. He always used to joke about serious things. I wish I had come <br>
back sooner, just to see him off.'<br>
 'I think really he preferred slipping off quietly in the <br>
end,' said Gandalf. 'Don't be too troubled. He'll be all right â now. <br>
He left a packet for you. There it is!'<br>
 Frodo took the envelope from the mantelpiece, and glanced at <br>
it, but did not open it.<br>
 'You'll find his will and all the other documents in there, I <br>
think,' said the wizard. 'You are the master of Bag End now. And <br>
also, I fancy, you'll find a golden ring.'<br>
 'The ring!' exclaimed Frodo. 'Has he left me that? I wonder <br>
why. Still, it may be useful.'<br>
 'It may, and it may not,' said Gandalf. 'I should not make <br>
use of it, if I were you. But keep it secret, and keep it safe! Now I <br>
am going to bed.'<br>
<br>
 As master of Bag End Frodo felt it his painful duty to say <br>
good-bye to the guests. Rumours of strange events had by now spread <br>
all over the field, but Frodo would only say no doubt everything will <br>
be cleared up in the morning. About midnight carriages came for the <br>
important folk. One by one they rolled away, filled with full but <br>
very unsatisfied hobbits. Gardeners came by arrangement, and removed <br>
in wheel-barrows those that had inadvertently remained behind.<br>
 Night slowly passed. The sun rose. The hobbits rose rather <br>
later. Morning went on. People came and began (by orders) to clear <br>
away the pavilions and the tables and the chairs, and the spoons and <br>
knives and bottles and plates, and the lanterns, and the flowering <br>
shrubs in boxes, and the crumbs and cracker-paper, the forgotten bags <br>
and gloves and handkerchiefs, and the uneaten food (a very small <br>
item). Then a number of other people came (without orders): <br>
Bagginses, and Boffins, and Bolgers, and Tooks, and other guests that <br>
lived or were staying near. By mid-day, when even the best-fed were <br>
out and about again, there was a large crowd at Bag End, uninvited <br>
but not unexpected.<br>
 Frodo was waiting on the step, smiling, but looking rather <br>
tired and worried. He welcomed all the callers, but he had not much <br>
more to say than before. His reply to all inquiries was simply <br>
this: 'Mr. Bilbo Baggins has gone away; as far as I know, for good.' <br>
Some of the visitors he invited to come inside, as Bilbo had <br>
left 'messages' for them.<br>
 Inside in the hall there was piled a large assortment of <br>
packages and parcels and small articles of furniture. On every item <br>
there was a label tied. There were several labels of this sort:<br>
 For ADELARD TOOK, for his VERY OWN, from Bilbo; on an <br>
umbrella. Adelard had carried off many unlabelled ones.<br>
 For DORA BAGGINS in memory of a LONG correspondence, with <br>
love from Bilbo; on a large waste-paper basket. Dora was Drogo's <br>
sister and the eldest surviving female relative of Bilbo and Frodo; <br>
she was ninety-nine, and had written reams of good advice for more <br>
than half a century.<br>
 For MILO BURROWS, hoping it will be useful, from B.B; on a <br>
gold pen and ink-bottle. Milo never answered letters.<br>
 For ANGELICA'S use, from Uncle Bilbo; on a round convex <br>
mirror. She was a young Baggins, and too obviously considered her <br>
face shapely.<br>
 For the collection of HUGO BRACEGIRDLE, from a contributor; <br>
on an (empty) book-case. Hugo was a great borrower of books, and <br>
worse than usual at returning them.<br>
 For LOBELIA SACKVILLE-BAGGINS, as a PRESENT; on a case of <br>
silver spoons. Bilbo believed that she had acquired a good many of <br>
his spoons, while he was away on his former journey. Lobelia knew <br>
that quite well. When she arrived later in the day, she took the <br>
point at once, but she also took the spoons.<br>
<br>
 This is only a small selection of the assembled presents. <br>
Bilbo's residence had got rather cluttered up with things in the <br>
course of his long life. It was a tendency of hobbit-holes to get <br>
cluttered up: for which the custom of giving so many birthday-<br>
presents was largely responsible. Not, of course, that the birthday-<br>
presents were always new; there were one or two old mathoms of <br>
forgotten uses that had circulated all around the district; but Bilbo <br>
had usually given new presents, and kept those that he received. The <br>
old hole was now being cleared a little.<br>
 Every one of the various parting gifts had labels, written <br>
out personally by Bilbo, and several had some point, or some joke. <br>
But, of course, most of the things were given where they would be <br>
wanted and welcome. The poorer hobbits, and especially those of <br>
Bagshot Row, did very well. Old Gaffer Gamgee got two sacks of <br>
potatoes, a new spade, a woollen waistcoat, and a bottle of ointment <br>
for creaking joints. Old Rory Brandybuck, in return for much <br>
hospitality, got a dozen bottles of Old Winyards: a strong red wine <br>
from the Southfarthing, and now quite mature, as it had been laid <br>
down by Bilbo's father. Rory quite forgave Bilbo, and voted him a <br>
capital fellow after the first bottle.<br>
 There was plenty of everything left for Frodo. And, of <br>
course, all the chief treasures, as well as the books, pictures, and <br>
more than enough furniture, were left in his possession. There was, <br>
however, no sign nor mention of money or jewellery: not a penny-piece <br>
or a glass bead was given away.<br>
<br>
 Frodo had a very trying time that afternoon. A false rumour <br>
that the whole household was being distributed free spread like <br>
wildfire; and before long the place was packed with people who had no <br>
business there, but could not be kept out. Labels got torn off and <br>
mixed, and quarrels broke out. Some people tried to do swaps and <br>
deals in the hall; and others tried to make off with minor items not <br>
addressed to them, or with anything that seemed unwanted or <br>
unwatched. The road to the gate was blocked with barrows and <br>
handcarts.<br>
 In the middle of the commotion the Sackville-Bagginses <br>
arrived. Frodo had retired for a while and left his friend Merry <br>
Brandybuck to keep an eye on things. When Otho loudly demanded to see <br>
Frodo, Merry bowed politely.<br>
 'He is indisposed,' he said. 'He is resting.'<br>
 'Hiding, you mean,' said Lobelia. 'Anyway we want to see him <br>
and we mean to see him. Just go and tell him so!'<br>
 Merry left them a long while in the hall, and they had time <br>
to discover their parting gift of spoons. It did not improve their <br>
tempers. Eventually they were shown into the study. Frodo was sitting <br>
at a table with a lot of papers in front of him. He looked <br>
indisposed â to see Sackville-Bagginses at any rate; and he stood up, <br>
fidgeting with something in his pocket. But he spoke quite politely.<br>
 The Sackville-Bagginses were rather offensive. They began by <br>
offering him bad bargain-prices (as between friends) for various <br>
valuable and unlabelled things. When Frodo replied that only the <br>
things specially directed by Bilbo were being given away, they said <br>
the whole affair was very fishy.<br>
 'Only one thing is clear to me,' said Otho, 'and that is that <br>
you are doing exceedingly well out of it. I insist on seeing the <br>
will.'<br>
 Otho would have been Bilbo's heir, but for the adoption of <br>
Frodo. He read the will carefully and snorted. It was, unfortunately, <br>
very clear and correct (according to the legal customs of hobbits, <br>
which demand among other things seven signatures of witnesses in red <br>
ink).<br>
 'Foiled again!' he said to his wife. 'And after waiting sixty <br>
years. Spoons? Fiddlesticks!' He snapped his fingers under Frodo's <br>
nose and stumped off. But Lobelia was not so easily got rid of. A <br>
little later Frodo came out of the study to see how things were going <br>
on and found her still about the place, investigating nooks and <br>
corners and tapping the floors. He escorted her firmly off the <br>
premises, after he had relieved her of several small (but rather <br>
valuable) articles that had somehow fallen inside her umbrella. Her <br>
face looked as if she was in the throes of thinking out a really <br>
crushing parting remark; but all she found to say, turning round on <br>
the step, was:<br>
 'You'll live to regret it, young fellow! Why didn't you go <br>
too? You don't belong here; you're no Baggins â you â you're a <br>
Brandybuck!'<br>
 'Did you hear that, Merry? That was an insult, if you like,' <br>
said Frodo as he shut the door on her.<br>
 'It was a compliment,' said Merry Brandybuck, 'and so, of <br>
course, not true.'<br>
 Then they went round the hole, and evicted three young <br>
hobbits (two Boffins and a Bolger) who were knocking holes in the <br>
walls of one of the cellars. Frodo also had a tussle with young <br>
Sancho Proudfoot (old Odo Proudfoot's grandson), who had begun an <br>
excavation in the larger pantry, where he thought there was an echo. <br>
The legend of Bilbo's gold excited both curiosity and hope; for <br>
legendary gold (mysteriously obtained, if not positively ill-gotten) <br>
is, as every one knows, any one's for the finding â unless the search <br>
is interrupted.<br>
 When he had overcome Sancho and pushed him out, Frodo <br>
collapsed on a chair in the hall. 'It's time to close the shop, <br>
Merry,' he said. 'Lock the door, and don't open it to anyone today, <br>
not even if they bring a battering ram.' Then he went to revive <br>
himself with a belated cup of tea.<br>
 He had hardly sat down, when there came a soft knock at the <br>
front-door. 'Lobelia again most likely,' he thought. 'She must have <br>
thought of something really nasty, and have come back again to say <br>
it. It can wait.'<br>
 He went on with his tea. The knock was repeated, much louder, <br>
but he took no notice. Suddenly the wizard's head appeared at the <br>
window.<br>
 'If you don't let me in, Frodo, I shall blow your door right <br>
down your hole and out through the hill,' he said.<br>
 'My dear Gandalf! Half a minute!' cried Frodo, running out of <br>
the room to the door. 'Come in! Come in! I thought it was Lobelia.'<br>
 'Then I forgive you. But I saw her some time ago, driving a <br>
pony-trap towards Bywater with a face that would have curdled new <br>
milk.'<br>
 'She had already nearly curdled me. Honestly, I nearly tried <br>
on Bilbo's ring. I longed to disappear.'<br>
 'Don't do that!' said Gandalf, sitting down. 'Do be careful <br>
of that ring, Frodo! In fact, it is partly about that that I have <br>
come to say a last word.'<br>
 'Well, what about it?'<br>
 'What do you know already?'<br>
 'Only what Bilbo told me. I have heard his story: how he <br>
found it, and how he used it: on his journey, I mean.'<br>
 'Which story, I wonder,' said Gandalf.<br>
 'Oh, not what he told the dwarves and put in his book,' said <br>
Frodo. 'He told me the true story soon after I came to live here. He <br>
said you had pestered him till he told you, so I had better know <br>
too. "No secrets between us, Frodo," he said; "but they are not to go <br>
any further. It's mine anyway."'<br>
 'That's interesting,' said Gandalf. 'Well, what did you think <br>
of it all?'<br>
 'If you mean, inventing all that about a "present", well, I <br>
thought the true story much more likely, and I couldn't see the point <br>
of altering it at all. It was very unlike Bilbo to do so, anyway; and <br>
I thought it rather odd.'<br>
 'So did I. But odd things may happen to people that have such <br>
treasures â if they use them. Let it be a warning to you to be very <br>
careful with it. It may have other powers than just making you vanish <br>
when you wish to.'<br>
 'I don't understand,' said Frodo.<br>
 'Neither do I,' answered the wizard. 'I have merely begun to <br>
wonder about the ring, especially since last night. No need to worry. <br>
But if you take my advice you will use it very seldom, or not at all. <br>
At least I beg you not to use it in any way that will cause talk or <br>
rouse suspicion. I say again: keep it safe, and keep it secret!'<br>
 'You are very mysterious! What are you afraid of?'<br>
 'I am not certain, so I will say no more. I may be able to <br>
tell you something when I come back. I am going off at once: so this <br>
is good-bye for the present.' He got up.<br>
 'At once!' cried Frodo. 'Why, I thought you were staying on <br>
for at least a week. I was looking forward to your help.'<br>
 'I did mean to â but I have had to change my mind. I may be <br>
away for a good while; but I'll come and see you again, as soon as I <br>
can. Expect me when you see me! I shall slip in quietly. I shan't <br>
often be visiting the Shire openly again. I find that I have become <br>
rather unpopular. They say I am a nuisance and a disturber of the <br>
peace. Some people are actually accusing me of spiriting Bilbo away, <br>
or worse. If you want to know, there is supposed to be a plot between <br>
you and me to get hold of his wealth.'<br>
 'Some people!' exclaimed Frodo. 'You mean Otho and Lobelia. <br>
How abominable! I would give them Bag End and everything else, if I <br>
could get Bilbo back and go off tramping in the country with him. I <br>
love the Shire. But I begin to wish, somehow, that I had gone too. I <br>
wonder if I shall ever see him again.'<br>
 'So do I,' said Gandalf. 'And I wonder many other things. <br>
Good-bye now! Take care of yourself! Look out for me, especially at <br>
unlikely times! Good-bye!'<br>
 Frodo saw him to the door. He gave a final wave of his hand, <br>
and walked off at a surprising pace; but Frodo thought the old wizard <br>
looked unusually bent, almost as if he was carrying a great weight. <br>
The evening was closing in, and his cloaked figure quickly vanished <br>
into the twilight. Frodo did not see him again for a long time.<br>

        <span itemprop="reviewRating" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Rating">

                        <span itemprop="name">All three books in one</span>
                    </h4>
                    
                            <p class="review_summary" itemprop="reviewBody">Since others were asking, it is all three books in one.</p>

<span id="ReportThisReview-rnd-1304844827" class="report-this-review reviewId-2426432"><span class="default" style=""><a href="http://www.barnesandnoble.com/reportreview/Lord-of-the-Rings%2FJ-R-R-Tolkien/1100188646"  alt="2426432" data-bntrack="Report" class="report_review" data-bn-csrfToken="cg270FO0QCYHqgRfl8I74X4FD7gxGVwt">Report this review</a></span><span class="submitted" style="">Thank you, this review has been flagged.</span></span>

            <li class="review-body box" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
                <div class="horiz_rule">
                    <div class="fade-in"></div>
                    <div class="fade-out"></div>
                </div>
                <div class="customer_info c15-3col">
                    
                            <p itemprop="author">Ifrost</p>
                        
                    <p>Posted <meta itemprop="datePublished" content="2011-05-25">Mai 25, 2011</p>
                    
                </div>
                <div class="review_content c15-11col c17-13col">
                    <h4 class="review_title strong">
                        
        <span itemprop="reviewRating" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Rating">
            <span class="stars-small r5" title="Average rating of 5 out of 5 stars"></span>
            <meta itemprop="ratingValue" content="5" />
        </span>
    
                        <span itemprop="name">Books vs. Movies</span>
                    </h4>
                    
                            <p class="review_summary" itemprop="reviewBody">I recently completed a course at a community college in California where we reviewed The Lord of the Rings Trilogy, The Hobbit, and the movies (all four movies, if you count the animated hobbit film as a movie, which I do only begrudgingly). This post is the final assignment in the class. We learned a bit about the author in the class, and I have more respect for Tolkien now. What I didn't know before was that Tolkien had already created middle earth in his mind, unlike many modern fantasy writers who seem to just throw together a generic map and give it some crazy name. In Tolkien's work there is a sense that the world is much older than the stories he is telling, it is less so with other writers who sometimes create two or more worlds, which seem only as a place for the story to take place. Middle Earth has a quality that makes it more than that, it's almost a character rather than a location. My first exposure to Tolkien was in anticipation of the first movie in 2000. I read the first book before the first movie came out, then completed the other two books before the second movie came out. I also read The Hobbit at some point. I enjoyed the movies enough that I only watched them a couple of times so as to preserve the enchantment. I was a young teenager when I first read the books, and I was too young to appreciate the good writing. At the time I preferred the movies over the books because I found the names and locations to be difficult to distinguish in reading, but easy to visualize with the movies. Having taken this course I now have a reversed opinion of the books and movies. I believe the movies are as good as can be done, but the literary work is much better. However, I am not a purist when it comes to movies vs. books. Reading and watching Lord of the Rings in conjunction with each other was a great experience because it gave two points of view of the same story. I would recommend that everyone with an interest in the series compare the two. It is pretty well accepted that movie renditions are never as good as the book they are based on, and out of necessity there are things altered to fit budget and time restraints, but if you can take those facts as read there are some interesting comparisons that can be made between the two media sources. For example, the character Faramir is completely different in the movie. Some people are content to say that the movie ruined Faramir, but getting past the differences helps to get a new view of the character. I used this practice with any apparant discrepancies between the book and movies. After ranting about how they messed up in the movie, I took an objective view and it helped me to value both works higher.</p>
                            
                                <p class="review_count"><span class="strong">92</span> out of <span class="strong">126</span> people found this review helpful.</p>

                        <span itemprop="name">Lord of the Rings is the Best Book Ever Written</span>
                    </h4>
                    
                            <p class="review_summary" itemprop="reviewBody">Lord of the Rings, a powerful title for a powerful book. The best masterpiece of all times Lord of the Rings takes you to a different world where anything is possible.<br/>              Lord of the Rings takes you to an accurately depicted land called Middle-Earth (he even draws a map for you). Here any imaginary creature imagined can be located (metaphor) including creatures like orcs, elves, dwarves, and wizards. They are cleverly described in there own unique form, and are made real in front of your eyes. He makes you hate, love, admire, and pity the characters to such extent that you won't be able to put the book down. This book contains everything: tragedy, love, treachery, action, adventure, etc., and is a book that must be read.<br/>             This book has been made a movie, and may be discouraged to reading it, but this would be a GREAT MISTAKE. The movie is great, I don't deny it, but it doesn't explain the whole book. The book is in the top ten best sellers, and the movies also. J.R.R. Tolkien put his life in this work (it even includes songs and poems), I have never seen a more complete work in my life, nothing is missing. This is truly a masterpiece full of characters, places, and stories.</p>

                    <h4 class="review_title strong">
                        
        <span itemprop="reviewRating" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Rating">
            <span class="stars-small r5" title="Average rating of 5 out of 5 stars"></span>
            <meta itemprop="ratingValue" content="5" />
        </span>
    
                        <span itemprop="name">Excellent Quality</span>
                    </h4>
                    
                            <p class="review_summary" itemprop="reviewBody">I am very pleased with this edition and would heartily recommend it to anyone looking for a single volume edition of The Lord of The Rings.<br/><br/>Starting with the down side - there is very little wrong with this volume.  I just finished reading the novels (not the appendixes) and found only two typos.  This is much better than average for texts scanned from a paper original.  I would also have preferred to have more of Tolkien's original illustrations included.  The exterior of my copy had rubber cement drips from the glueing of the fold out maps.  They came off but were a little disappointing in a book of this quality.<br/><br/>On the positive side - The binding of the volume is consistent with its price.  The leather is soft and pleasant to the touch.  The ribbon page marker is a nice convenience for such a large volume.  The fold out maps are very nice and a big plus for a first time reader trying to understand Middle Earth.  The high gloss photographs depicting the book of Mazarbul are a nice bonus.  The weight and substance of the book are fitting for the chapters of long and dreary walking.  The leather and the gilt edges lend to the sense of majesty and consequence captured by the writing.  A fitting tribute to the text itself.</p>

                <div class="review_content c15-11col c17-13col">
                    <h4 class="review_title strong">
                        
        <span itemprop="reviewRating" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Rating">
            <span class="stars-small r5" title="Average rating of 5 out of 5 stars"></span>
            <meta itemprop="ratingValue" content="5" />
        </span>
    
                        <span itemprop="name">Great new release of one of the world's greatest novels of all time</span>
                    </h4>
                    
                            <p class="review_summary" itemprop="reviewBody">This hardcover one-volume edition of Lord of the Rings was released for the 50th anniversary of its publication (1954).  Over the years, numerous &quot;corrections&quot; in the spelling of certain words by well-intentioned publishers and printers led to some confusion among readers.  They weren't really serious mistakes but some readers were left confused by the lack of consistency in the text.  They never really took away from the beauty of the novel, but Tolkien was constantly frustrated by these constantly reoccurring errors.  This is supposed to be the definitive edition with all mistakes corrected, without altering anything significant in the text or really changing what the author himself wrote.<br/><br/>It's printed on good glossyish paper and the type is easy to read.  I've carried the book around for a while and it isn't falling apart at the seams.  It seems to be bound well enough to last a while.<br/><br/>The *only* shortcoming that this edition of the Lord of the Rings has, in my opinion, is the lack of fold-out maps that appear in the collectors editions and the three volume editions of the novel (and that is what it is: a novel and NOT a trilogy).  Instead, it shows four different maps, as if the original map of middle earth were divided into quarters at the center, but they overlap for convenience.  It's not a bad thing but I think for the price the publisher really could have included a fold-out map or two.  But that's the only drawback whatsoever and his original map of the Shire at the beginning of book one is still there intact.<br/><br/>As for the novel itself, what a great, epic tale.  I really only wanted this review to explain the new edition and what makes it good since so many already know the story behind the Lord of the Rings.  The movies, to get off topic for a moment, were actually pretty faithful to the book in many respects.  But even if you've seen the movies you haven't seen nothin' yet until you have read the book (which is quite often the case with great novels that get made into movies).  My favorite character is Sam, Frodo's faithful companion.  While book one starts out a bit slow, it picks up the pace considerably in book two and really gets into the action.  Yes, it's long, but it's as just about as long as it needs to be.<br/><br/>This hardcover edition is well worth the price, and more convenient to read than a three volume edition, where you'd have to keep flipping from one volume to the next if you were to refer to the index or appendices that follow volume three.  I recommend getting this 50th anniversary hardcover edition (or at a lesser cost, the paperback version of it).  I do not regret a single penny I paid for it.</p>
                            
                                <p class="review_count"><span class="strong">13</span> out of <span class="strong">18</span> people found this review helpful.</p>

        <span itemprop="reviewRating" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Rating">

                        <span itemprop="name">A Must Have</span>
                    </h4>
                    
                            <p class="review_summary" itemprop="reviewBody">I loved this book so much, that I just had to get the Red Leather Edition and the New 50th Ann. Black  edition(Though I thought it was blue from the small photo online). I also have the great green leather 'There and Back Again' (The Hobbit). When other J.R.R. books come along in Leather, I'll add that to my collection... which I hope other books such as 'The Silmarillion' will be shortly.  I suggest reading the book before seeing the movie, or do somewhat of a review book/movie (i.e. to see what they left out - think of your own reasons why, besides the movie would have been more than 3 hours long per 'episode' and odd characters).</p>
                            
                                <p class="review_count"><span class="strong">8</span> out of <span class="strong">10</span> people found this review helpful.</p>
                            














<span id="WasThisHelpful-rnd-1674677194" class="was-this-helpful reviewId-614536"><span class="default" style="">Was this review helpful? <a href="https://www.barnesandnoble.com/signin/popup"  alt="614536" data-bntrack="HelpfulYes" class="helpful-trigger" data-bn-csrfToken="cg270FO0QCYHqgRfl8I74X4FD7gxGVwt">Yes</a><span class="vert-sep">&nbsp;</span> <a href="https://www.barnesandnoble.com/signin/popup"  alt="614536" data-bntrack="HelpfulNo" class="helpful-trigger" data-bn-csrfToken="cg270FO0QCYHqgRfl8I74X4FD7gxGVwt">No</a></span><span class="submitted" style="">Thank you for your feedback.</span></span>
